The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%20%283%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. Might be OK, might be a smell, Dunno atm.\n\nGimme more review-time ;)')::text, DEFAULT) ON CONFLICT ON CONSTRAINT \"vcs_PullRequestComment_pkey\" DO UPDATE SET \"origin\" = {constraint: vcs_PullRequestComment_pkey, update_columns: [author, comment, createdAt, number, origin, updatedAt]}) { id } m54: insert_vcs_PullRequestComment_one (object: {number: 1, uid: "1", comment: "Soooo, I guess we all can learn a lot from the overall changes proposed within this PR.\nDidn´t invest much time in actual reviewing this here, but some parts immediately pop into my eyes, which should rellay be addressed prior this should be merged here.\nI´ll just concentrate on a single aspect here for now.\n\nI recognize that you applied some typings here and there. \nTHe problem with what I saw was, that many places tend to introduce typings on the calling-site of e.g. on the return a method, rather than relying on thy `typings`of the `providing-site`.\n\ne.g.: [here](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/cea8cb49ca83b273809b9c1d8746d068fc780d1e?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport) and [there](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/ee23032f71bf3e720abe506f0ed34089ce8389f8?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport).\n\nThis is kind of problematic, as the code literally states \"whatever may return from that call, I´ll treat it as XYZ, no matter what\".\nLet me try to visualize that with a maybe stupid analogy, which I just came up with, spontaneously:\n\n# Analogy - The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image {constraint: vcs_PullRequestComment_pkey, update_columns: [author, comment, createdAt, number, origin, updatedAt]}) { id } m26: insert_vcs_PullRequestComment_one (object: {number: 1, uid: "1", comment: "Soooo, I guess we all can learn a lot from the overall changes proposed within this PR.\nDidn´t invest much time in actual reviewing this here, but some parts immediately pop into my eyes, which should rellay be addressed prior this should be merged here.\nI´ll just concentrate on a single aspect here for now.\n\nI recognize that you applied some typings here and there. \nTHe problem with what I saw was, that many places tend to introduce typings on the calling-site of e.g. on the return a method, rather than relying on thy `typings`of the `providing-site`.\n\ne.g.: [here](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/cea8cb49ca83b273809b9c1d8746d068fc780d1e?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport) and [there](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/ee23032f71bf3e720abe506f0ed34089ce8389f8?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport).\n\nThis is kind of problematic, as the code literally states \"whatever may return from that call, I´ll treat it as XYZ, no matter what\".\nLet me try to visualize that with a maybe stupid analogy, which I just came up with, spontaneously:\n\n# Analogy - The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%20%283%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. 'Azure-Repos|Alexander.Clare@vwfsdev.com', DEFAULT, ('2023-02-17T19:38:45.91Z')::timestamptz, ('1')::bigint, ('Soooo, I guess we all can learn a lot from the overall changes proposed within this PR.\nDidn´t invest much time in actual reviewing this here, but some parts immediately pop into my eyes, which should rellay be addressed prior this should be merged here.\nI´ll just concentrate on a single aspect here for now.\n\nI recognize that you applied some typings here and there. \nTHe problem with what I saw was, that many places tend to introduce typings on the calling-site of e.g. on the return a method, rather than relying on thy `typings`of the `providing-site`.\n\ne.g.: [here](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/cea8cb49ca83b273809b9c1d8746d068fc780d1e?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport) and [there](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/ee23032f71bf3e720abe506f0ed34089ce8389f8?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport).\n\nThis is kind of problematic, as the code literally states \"whatever may return from that call, I´ll treat it as XYZ, no matter what\".\nLet me try to visualize that with a maybe stupid analogy, which I just came up with, spontaneously:\n\n# Analogy - The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%%283%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. Might be OK, might be a smell, Dunno atm.\n\nGimme more review-time ;)')::text, DEFAULT) ON CONFLICT ON CONSTRAINT \"vcs_PullRequestComment_pkey\" DO UPDATE SET \"origin\" = comment: "Soooo, I guess we all can learn a lot from the overall changes proposed within this PR.\nDidn´t invest much time in actual reviewing this here, but some parts immediately pop into my eyes, which should rellay be addressed prior this should be merged here.\nI´ll just concentrate on a single aspect here for now.\n\nI recognize that you applied some typings here and there. \nTHe problem with what I saw was, that many places tend to introduce typings on the calling-site of e.g. on the return a method, rather than relying on thy `typings`of the `providing-site`.\n\ne.g.: [here](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/cea8cb49ca83b273809b9c1d8746d068fc780d1e?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport) and [there](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/ee23032f71bf3e720abe506f0ed34089ce8389f8?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport).\n\nThis is kind of problematic, as the code literally states \"whatever may return from that call, I´ll treat it as XYZ, no matter what\".\nLet me try to visualize that with a maybe stupid analogy, which I just came up with, spontaneously:\n\n# Analogy - The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%20%283%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. Might be OK, might be a smell, Dunno atm.\n\nGimme more review-time ;)", createdAt: "2023-02-17T19:38:45.910Z", updatedAt: "2023-02-17T19:38:45.910Z", vcs_User: {data: {uid: The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. single aspect here for now.\n\nI recognize that you applied some typings here and there. \nTHe problem with what I saw was, that many places tend to introduce typings on the calling-site of e.g. on the return a method, rather than relying on thy `typings`of the `providing-site`.\n\ne.g.: [here](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/GisAtWeb/_git/Analytics/commit/cea8cb49ca83b273809b9c1d8746d068fc780d1e?refName=refs/heads/22963-typeScriptSupport) and literally states \"whatever may return from that call, I´ll treat it as XYZ, no matter what\".\nLet me try to visualize that with a maybe stupid analogy, which I just came up with, spontaneously:\n\n# Analogy - The postman (or - woman or whatver a gender conform term would be ;) )\nImagine your a postman.\nYour task is to deliver `letters` and `packages` to receipients.\nYou go to pickup those goods.\n## Case 1: the stupid postman (a.ka. type-assumption)\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something (which happens to be a `letter`).\nUpon retrieval, you decide to just treat this something as a `letter`.\nThen you get handed another something  (which happens to be a `package`).\nAs you´re not told otherwise, you again treat it as a `letter` (neglecting the fact that you´re a human with tremendious pattern-matching capabilities)\nNext, you know that you have to recieve at least a single `package` to be delivered.\nThe other employee hands you another something (which happens to be a `letter`, but which you decide - upon your expectation -, to be a `pacakge`)\n\nThat´s it, no more goods to pickup, so you start delivering those pieces.\n\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the receipient.\n\n2. Second Receipient: No matter what, you just don´t seeem to fit this f*****ng `letter`(which you assumed the `something` to be) in that darn letter-box. You really try hard, but as soon as you notice something shattering inside that `something` (wich you assumed to be a `letter`), you decide to \"let it go\" and continue your  journey.\n\n3. Third site to visit, you know you have only a single `package `left in your trunk (your assumption HAS to be right, though), and that `package` needs to be delivered directly to the customer (or a neighbour). You have been trained to act like this, so, get things going!\n\n4. Upon arrival, you hit the ringing-bell, a couple of times. Sadly, nobody opens the door. So you ring another bell (thank god for those trainings), and another, and another. Finally, you succeed to get the well deserved attention your job implies, and somebody opens the frontdoor for you. For your frustration, the person which kindly opens the frontdoor for you, lives at level 8 of a huge building, that sadly, sadly has a broken elevator. So you start walking up those stairs, sacrificing those precious minutes of early quitting time for the sake of duty.\nOnce you arrive on that cursed level, you hand over that `something` (which you assumed to be a `package`), which originally was intended for a person that lives on level 1 and that really would have been able to be put into an ordinary letter-box on level 0. Upon receiving this `somethign` the person (at level 8) just says:\"Well, nice...that´s some kind of vocation I´ve never been seen before\", and promises to hand over that `letter` (which you assumed to be a `package`  that **has** to be delivered in person) as soon as possible. \n\n5. Frustrated about your day, knowing to fill out those hated \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" has to be filled out, sooner or later. Otherwise your boss is getting real annoying...\n\nFrustrating day, indeed. Let´s see what could have been gone better:\n\n## Case 2: the smart postman (a.ka. type-assurance (or inferrence))\nAt the counter, the other employee hands you over something and tells you that´s a `letter` (which happens to be a `letter`).\nThen you get handed another something, which your colleague identifies as a `package` (which happens to be a `package`).\nNext, you really don´t know what to expect, but your colleague assures you to be a `letter`.\n\n1. At arrival at site A, you successfully deliver that `letter` into the letter-box of the designated receipient.\n2. Second Receipient: You ring the bell, as `package`s have to be deliverd in person. The door opens, you advance to the 3rd floor and hand over the precious goods inside this `package`\n3. Third site to visit, you just drop this leightweight letter into the receipients letter-box, regardless of the fact if the person it at home atm or not.\n4. Time for relaxation. No \"Exceptional Report during Delivery of Mail and Package delivery report\" to be filled out today. Nice!\n\n\n# Notice the difference?\n## Case 1 \nDoes exaclty what the provided code does: making assumptions about values retreived.\nAs this might kind-of silence the compiler, there might be various problems arising during runtime.\nConsider the following call:\n\n```typescript\nconst blobClient: BlobClient = containerClient.getBlobClient(oldName);\n```\nYou´re acting as the `stupid postman`: no matter what this method might return, You´ll treat it as a `BlobClient`.\nThis might be OK in most of the scenarios, but you have actually no controll over what the said method might **really** return. Far better would be to let the type be `inferred`, and warn or error you, if the following method invocations have some issues (`put package into letter-box`)  This is especially true in situations where the implementation might change a lot during development, e.g. within a local project, modified by other colleagues, constantly.\n\nCase 2:\nAs the provided code-sample from \"Case 1\" might impose: \"WTF, this is just a library I´ve called, and there´s no better way!\", one could be right, if not the package-authors would provide said `typings` to be available for people to consume (which for the vast majority of npm-packages usually is true, even if one might have to install an according `@typings`-package`\n## former example\n![image (3).png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image%20%283%29.png) \n## better example\n![image.png](https://dev.azure.com/vwfs/55bf3504-405f-46ad-a342-fb8edcf4913f/_apis/git/repositories/be60230a-a534-44a1-ab3e-c558b7972661/pullRequests/9069/attachments/image.png) \nThe type inferred is already the most discrete type.\n\nIn other words: The library already tells you, what resulting type you have to expect!\n\nIf, for example, the library authors decide to return another possible ype, the code of `former example`might break, as it is not in any means aware of that change, as it treats everything returning from `getContainerClient` as the type `ContainerClient`, even if it may end up being a `SomethingOriginallyIncompatibleWithContainerClient`...\nNote that this only probably may a pitfall we as devs might suffer from, as library authors tend to be aware of what callers might expect...but this kind of pitfall can be circumvented, by just applying simple rules:\n## don´t assume, assure!\n \n# Other stuff\n\n`eslint-disable-next-line consistent-return`\nI currently cannot grasp why those are actually needed in the first place. Might be OK, might be a smell, Dunno atm.\n\nGimme more review-time ;)", createdAt: "2023-02-17T19:38:45.910Z", updatedAt: "2023-02-17T19:38:45.910Z", vcs_User: {data: {uid:
